<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Exceptional subgenome stability and functional divergence in the allotetraploid Ethiopian cereal teff"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-02-14"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Teff (Eragrostis tef) is a cornerstone of food security in the Horn of Africa, where it is prized for stress resilience, grain nutrition, and market value. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of allotetraploid teff (variety Dabbi) and patterns of subgenome dynamics. The teff genome contains two complete sets of homoeologous chromosomes, with most genes maintaining as syntenic gene pairs. TE analysis allows us to estimate that the teff polyploidy event occurred ~1.1 million years ago (mya) and that the two subgenomes diverged ~5.0 mya. Despite this divergence, we detect no large-scale structural rearrangements, homoeologous exchanges, or biased gene loss, in contrast to many other allopolyploids. The two teff subgenomes have partitioned their ancestral functions based on divergent expression across a diverse expression atlas. Together, these genomic resources will be useful for accelerating breeding of this underutilized grain crop and for fundamental insights into polyploid genome evolution. Teff is an indigenous cereal critical to food security in the Horn of Africa. Here, the authors report an improved genome assembly and observe the surprisingly low levels of large-scale structural rearrangement, homoeologous exchanges, or bias gene loss after the formation of this tetraploid species."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Teff (<i>Eragrostis tef</i>) is a cornerstone of food security in the Horn of Africa, where it is prized for stress resilience, grain nutrition, and market value. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of allotetraploid teff (variety Dabbi) and patterns of subgenome dynamics. The teff genome contains two complete sets of homoeologous chromosomes, with most genes maintaining as syntenic gene pairs. TE analysis allows us to estimate that the teff polyploidy event occurred ~1.1 million years ago (mya) and that the two subgenomes diverged ~5.0 mya. Despite this divergence, we detect no large-scale structural rearrangements, homoeologous exchanges, or biased gene loss, in contrast to many other allopolyploids. The two teff subgenomes have partitioned their ancestral functions based on divergent expression across a diverse expression atlas. Together, these genomic resources will be useful for accelerating breeding of this underutilized grain crop and for fundamental insights into polyploid genome evolution.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Thirty crop species supply over 90% of the worldâs food needs and this narrow diversity reduces global food security. Humans have domesticated several hundred distinct plant species, but most are underutilized, under-improved, and restricted to their regions of origin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Mueller, N. G., Fritz, G. J., Patton, P., Carmody, S. &amp; Horton, E. T. Growing the lost crops of eastern North Americaâs original agricultural system. Nature Plants 3, 17092 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d58041e658">1</a></sup>. Although food systems have become increasingly diverse in the past few decades, many locally adapted species have been replaced by calorically dense staple crops, resulting in global homogeneity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Khoury, C. K. et al. Increasing homogeneity in global food supplies and the implications for food security. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 4001â4006 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d58041e662">2</a></sup>. Many underutilized and orphan crop species have desirable nutritional profiles, abiotic and biotic stress resilience, and untapped genetic potential for feeding our growing populations during this period of rapidly changing climate.</p><p>Teff is the staple grain crop in Ethiopia, and it is preferred over other cereals because of its nutritional profile, low input demand, adaptability, and cultural significance. Unlike other major cereals, teff is grown primarily by small-scale, subsistence farmers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="CSA. Agricultural Sample Survey 2011/2012: Report on Area and Production of Major Crops (Central Statistical Agency Addis Ababa, 2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d58041e669">3</a></sup> and thousands of locally adapted cultivars have been developed. Teff is among the most resilient cereals, tolerating marginal and semi-arid soils that are unsuitable for wheat, maize, sorghum, or rice production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Stallknecht, G. F., Gilbertson, K. M. &amp; Eckhoff, J. in New crops 231â234 (Wiley, New York, 1993)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d58041e673">4</a></sup>. Teff was likely domesticated in the northern Ethiopian Highlands where much of the genetic diversity can be found<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Demissie, A. in Narrowing the Rift. Tef Research and Development. Proc. International Workshop on Tef Genetics and Improvement (Debre Zeit, Ethiopia, 2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d58041e677">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="DâAndrea, A. C. Tâef (Eragrostis tef) in ancient agricultural systems of highland Ethiopia. Econ. Bot. 62, 547â566 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d58041e680">6</a></sup>. Consistent yields of small, nutritious seeds were the primary domestication targets of teff, contrasting most cereals where large seed heads and high productivity under tillage were desirable<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="DâAndrea, A. C. Tâef (Eragrostis tef) in ancient agricultural systems of highland Ethiopia. Econ. Bot. 62, 547â566 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d58041e684">6</a></sup>. Despite its stress tolerance, yield improvements lag behind other cereals because of issues related to lodging, seed shattering, extreme drought, and poor agronomic practices<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Abraham, B. et al. The system of crop intensification: reports from the field on improving agricultural production, food security, and resilience to climate change for multiple crops. Agric. Food Security 3, 4 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d58041e688">7</a></sup>. Teff and other orphan cereals have undergone limited intensive selection for high productivity under ideal conditions, and rapid gains should be possible with advanced breeding and genome selection. A rough draft genome is available for the teff cultivar Tsedey (DZ-Cr-37)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cannarozzi, G. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequencing identifies breeding targets in the orphan crop tef (Eragrostis tef). BMC Genomics 15, 581 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58041e693">8</a></sup>, but the utility of these sequence data are limited because of the assemblyâs fragmented and incomplete nature.</p><p>The wild progenitor of teff is likely <i>Eragrostis pilosa</i>, a hardy wild grass sharing considerable overlap in morphological, genetic, and karyotype traits with teff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gugsa, L. et al. in Narrowing the Rift: Tef Research and development. Proc. International Workshop on Tef Genetics and Improvement (Debre Zeit, Ethiopia, 2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d58041e703">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ingram, A. L. &amp; Doyle, J. J. The origin and evolution of Eragrostis tef (Poaceae) and related polyploids: evidence from nuclear waxy and plastid rps16. Am. J. Bot. 90, 116â122 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d58041e706">10</a></sup>. <i>Eragrostis tef</i> and <i>E. pilosa</i> are allotretraploids that arose from a shared polyploidy event that merged two currently unknown and possibly extinct or unsampled diploid genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ingram, A. L. &amp; Doyle, J. J. The origin and evolution of Eragrostis tef (Poaceae) and related polyploids: evidence from nuclear waxy and plastid rps16. Am. J. Bot. 90, 116â122 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d58041e716">10</a></sup>. Many crop plants are polyploid, and genome doubling can give rise to emergent traits such as spinnable fibers in cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d58041e721">11</a></sup>, morphological diversity in <i>Brassica</i> sp.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Osborn, T. C. The contribution of polyploidy to variation in Brassica species. Physiol. Plant. 121, 531â536 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d58041e728">12</a></sup>, and new aromatic profiles of strawberry fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ulrich, D. &amp; Olbricht, K. Diversity of volatile patterns in sixteen Fragaria vesca L. accessions in comparison to cultivars of FragariaÃ ananassa. J. Appl. Bot. Food Qual. 86, 36â46 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d58041e732">13</a></sup>. Successful establishment of allopolyploids requires coordination of two distinct sets of homoeologous genes and networks, and often a dominant subgenome emerges to resolve genetic and epigenetic conflicts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 131â139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d58041e736">14</a></sup>. Newly formed polyploids are often unstable and undergo numerous structural rearrangements and fractionation compared to their diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d58041e740">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Origin and evolution of the octoploid strawberry genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 541â547 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58041e743">16</a></sup>. Homeologous exchange is common during early polyploid formation, and large chromosome segments from one subgenome can replace another as observed in canola (<i>Brassica napus</i>), strawberry (<i>Fragaria ananassa</i>), cotton, and proso millet. The cotton allotetraploid complex formed around the same time as teff (1.7â1.9 million years ago (mya)), and the cotton A and D subgenomes diverged 6.2â7.1âmya. The two subgenomes have several hundred megabases of translocated sequences and strucural rearrangements. This same pattern of rearrangement is observed in the banana (<i>Musa balbisiana</i>) A and B subgenomes, which diverged ~5.4âmya and have several megabase pair sized translocations and inversions between them. The allohexaploid false flax (<i>Camelina stativa</i>) has evidence of shattered chromosomes with numerous rearrangements and fractionation of the subgenomes compared to the diploid progenitors.</p><p>The effect of polyploidy on desirable traits and interactions between the two subgenomes remains untested in teff. Polyploidy is found in more than 90% of species within the grass subfamily containing teff (Chloridoideae), and this has been hypothesized to contribute to the stress tolerance and diversification of these grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Roodt, R. &amp; Spies, J. J. Chromosome studies in the grass subfamily Chloridoideae. II. An analysis of polyploidy. Taxon 52, 736â746 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d58041e763">17</a></sup>. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of the teff A and B subgenomes and test for patterns of subgenome interactions and divergence.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>We built a chromosome-scale assembly of the allotetraploid teff genome using a combination of long read single-molecule real-time sequencing and long-range high-throughput chromatin capture (Hi-C). In total, we generated 5.5 million filtered PacBio reads collectively spanning 52.9âGb or 85Ã coverage of the estimated 622âMb genome from the important teff landrace Dabbi. PacBio reads were error corrected and assembled using Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d58041e779">18</a></sup> and the resulting contigs were polished to remove residual errors with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PloS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58041e783">19</a></sup> using high coverage Illumina data (45Ã). The PacBio assembly has a contig N50 of 1.55âMb across 1344 contigs with a total assembly size of 576âMb; 92.6% of the estimated genome size. The average nucleotide identity between homoeologous gene regions in teff is 93.9%, and this high sequence divergence facilitated accurate phasing and assembly. We utilized 20 random fosmids to assess the accuracy of the PacBio-based assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">1</a>). The fosmids collectively span 351âkb and have an average identity of 99.9% to the teff genome with individual fosmids ranging from 99.3 to 100%. This suggests that our assembly is mostly complete and accurately polished.</p><p>Contigs from the Canu-based draft genome were anchored into a chromosome-scale assembly using a Hi-C-based scaffolding approach. After filtering, 20 high-confidence clusters were identified, consistent with the haploid chromosome number of teff (2<i>n</i>â=â40; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig1">1</a>). In total, 687 contigs collectively spanning 96% of the assembly (555âMb) were anchored and oriented across the 20 pseudomolecules (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Tab1">1</a>). Pseudomolecules ranged in size from 19 to 40âMb, consistent with the teff karyotype. Seven chimeric contigs corresponding to joined telomeres were identified and split based on Hi-C interactions. This chromosome scale version is referred to as tef V3.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Hi-C-based clustering of the teff genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Hi-C-based clustering of the teff genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="670" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Heat map showing the density of Hi-C interactions between contigs, with red indicating high density of interactions. Distinct chromosomes are highlighted by blue boxes and homoeologous chromosome pairs are numbered.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary statistics of the teff genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We assessed the accuracy of the pseudomolecule construction using a high-density single-nucleotide polmorphism (SNP)-based genetic map with 2002 markers across 32 linkage groups. This map was constructed using a recombinant inbred population derived from an interspecific cross of <i>E. tef</i> and <i>E. pilosa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yu, J.-K. et al. A genetic linkage map for tef [Eragrostis tef (Zucc.) Trotter]. Theor. Appl. Genet. 113, 1093â1102 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1132">20</a></sup>. The pseudomolecules and genetic map are highly collinear with an average Pearsonâs correlation coefficient between marker and physical distance of <i>Ï</i>â=â0.932 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig2">2</a>). Several chromosomes are broken into multiple linkage groups because of low maker density and these linkage groups can be joined based on physical location on the pseudomolecules. There were some marker incongruences between homeologous linkage groups, but this was generally low, suggesting that the tef A and B subgenomes are accurately phased and assembled.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Collinearity of tef pseudomolecules with the high-density genetic map."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Collinearity of tef pseudomolecules with the high-density genetic map.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="847" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Two example chromosomes demonstrate a pseudomolecule spanning three linkage groups (top) and a pseudomolecule spanning a single linkage group (bottom). Lines connect the genetic makers with their physical location on the pseudomolecules. <i>p</i> Values within the scatterplots indicate the Pearsonâs correlation coefficient of marker distance (cM) and physical distance (bp). Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The teff genome was annotated using the MAKER pipeline. Transcript support from a large-scale expression atlas and protein homology to <i>Arabidopsis</i> and other grass genomes were used as evidence for ab initio gene prediction. After filtering transposon-derived sequences, ab initio gene prediction identified 68,255 gene models. We assessed the annotation quality using the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Ortholog (BUSCO) Embryophyta dataset. The annotation contains 98.1% of the 1440 core Embryophyta genes and the majority (1210) areÂ found in duplicate in the A and B subgenomes.</p><p>The teff cultivar Tsedey (DZ-Cr-37) was previously sequenced using an Illumina-based approach, yielding a highly fragmented draft genome with 14,057 scaffolds and 50,006 gene models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cannarozzi, G. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequencing identifies breeding targets in the orphan crop tef (Eragrostis tef). BMC Genomics 15, 581 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1164">8</a></sup>. The fragmented nature of this assembly and incomplete annotation hinders downstream functional genomics, genetics, and marker-assisted breeding of teff. We compared the Tsedey assembly with our Dabbi reference to identify cultivar-specific genes and differences in assembly quality. Only 30,424 (60.8%) of the Tsedey gene models had similarity (&gt;95% sequence identity) to gene models in our Dabbi reference, including 9866 homoeologous gene pairs. Only 20,208 (29.6%) of our Dabbi gene models had homology to Tsedey gene models. The remaining gene models were unannotated or unassembled in the Tsedey assembly. Only one-third of the Tsedey genome is assembled into scaffolds large enough to be classified as syntenic blocks to Dabbi, which is an unavoidable artifact of the poor assembly quality and low contiguity. Because of the fragmented nature of the Tsedey assembly, we were unable to identify lineage-specific genes. Hence, the genomic resources presented here represent a significant advance over previous efforts.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Subgenome characteristics</h3><p>Teff is an allotetraploid with unknown diploid progenitors, but the polyploidy event is likely shared with other closely related <i>Eragrostis</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ingram, A. L. &amp; Doyle, J. J. The origin and evolution of Eragrostis tef (Poaceae) and related polyploids: evidence from nuclear waxy and plastid rps16. Am. J. Bot. 90, 116â122 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1179">10</a></sup>. Because the diploid progenitors are unknown and possibly extinct, we utilized the putative centromeric array sequences to distinguish the homoeologous chromosomes from the A and B subgenomes of teff. Putative centromeric (SatT) repeat arrays in teff range from 3.7 to 326âkb in size for each chromosome and individual arrays contain 22 to 824 copies (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">2</a>). We identified two distinct SatT arrays in teff (hereon referred to as SatTA and SatTB). SatTA and SatTB are the same length (159âbp) but have different sequence composition (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">1b</a>). This element was previously identified in Illumina short read data from the teff variety Enantite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Gebre, Y. G., Bertolini, E., PÃ¨, M. E. &amp; Zuccolo, A. Identification and characterization of abundant repetitive sequences in Eragrostis tef cv. Enatite genome. BMC Plant Biol. 16, 39 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1189">21</a></sup>. Alignment of the consensus SatT arrays identified several distinguishing polymorphisms and a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree separated the SatT arrays into two well-supported clades (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">1a</a>). Each clade contains one member from each of the ten homoeologous chromosome pairs and this classification likely represents differences in SatT array composition between the diploid progenitor species. This approach allowed us to accurately distinguish homoeologous chromosome pairs from the A and B subgenomes and verifies the allopolyploid origin of teff. This result is independently confirmed by an analysis that investigated historical transposable element (TE) activity (see below).</p><p>The teff subgenomes have 93.9% sequence similarity in the coding regions, suggesting that either the polyploidy event was relatively ancient or that the progenitor diploid species were highly divergent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Egan, A. N. Dating the origins of polyploidy events. N. Phytologist 186, 73â85 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1200">22</a></sup>. To estimate the divergence time of the A and B subgenomes, we calculated Ks (synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) between homoeologous gene pairs. Teff homoeologs have a single Ks peak with a median of 0.15 (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">2</a>), corresponding to a divergence time of ~5 million years based on a widely used mutation rate for grasses (1.5âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> substitutions per nonsynonymous site per year)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B. S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43â45 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1209">23</a></sup>. The ten pairs of homoeologous chromosomes are highly syntenic with no large-scale structural rearrangements. The A subgenome is 13% (37âMb) larger in size, but contains only 5% more genes than the B subgenome (34,032 vs. 32,255; TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Tab1">1</a>). Most genes (54,846) are maintained as homoeologous pairs and 13,409 are found in only one subgenome. Of these single-copy genes, 9036 have corresponding sequences in the homeologous chromosomes as either pseudogenes with frameshift mutations and missing exons, or low confidence gene models that were excluded from the final annotation. In total, ~93.5% of genes are maintained as homeologous gene pairs or a gene and pseudogene pair, with comparatively few being absent or deleted from one of the subgenomes. We identified 6876 tandemly duplicated genes with array sizes ranging from 2 to 15 copies. Of the 2748 tandem arrays, 998 are found in both subgenomes, while 864 and 1008 occur in only the A and B subgenomes, respectively (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Tab1">1</a>). Copy number varies extensively in shared arrays between the subgenomes.</p><p>The monoploid genome size of teff is relatively small (~300âMb) compared to other polyploid grasses, and repetitive elements constitute a low percentage (25.6%) of the genome. Long terminal repeat-retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) are the most abundant repetitive elements, spanning at least 115.9âMb or ~20.0% of the genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Tab2">2</a>). This predicted percentage is somewhat lower than that reported for other small grass genomes, such as Oropetium (250âMb; 27%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="VanBuren, R., Wai, C. M., Keilwagen, J. &amp; Pardo, J. A chromosome-scale assembly of the model desiccation tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Plant Direct 2, e00096 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1226">24</a></sup> and Brachypodium (272âMb; 21.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Initiative, I. B. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1230">25</a></sup>. We classified LTRs into 65 families and compared their abundance and insertion times (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig3">3</a>). A particular window of activity was seen for six families of LTR-RTs that were active only in the A genome progenitor or the B genome progenitor (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">3</a>). The insertion times for these genome-specific LTR-RTs were all greater than 1.1âmya, indicating the two subgenomes were evolving independently during this period. Hence, this LTR-RT analysis both confirms the A and B genome designations, and provides a methodology for determining the date of polyploid formation. In teff, these data indicate that the ancestral polyploidy was established ~1.1âmya.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table"
