elements are plotted. Families 1â5 have â¥100 intact LTRs, 6â33 have â¥10 LTRs, and 34â65 have â¥5 LTRs. The exact number of LTR-RTs in each family is available in the TE annotation gff file. The six subgenome-specific families are highlighted in blue and the estimated range for the teff polyploidy event is shown in brown. A substitution rate of 1.3e-8 per site per year was used to infer the element insertion times. Box boundaries indicate the 25th and 75th percentiles of the insertion time and whiskers extend to 1.5 times the interquartile range.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Five of the six subgenome-specific LTR-RT families were found only in the A subgenome, suggesting that LTR-RTs accumulate more rapidly in the A subgenome or are purged more effectively in the B subgenome. The A subgenome contains 35% more repetitive DNA than the B subgenome (87.5 vs. 64.9âMb) and the recent bursts of LTR-RT activity contributes to the 13% larger size of the A subgenome. There are 24 families with median insertion times between 1.1 and 2.4âmya, and the remaining 18 families do not exhibit subgenomic specificity. Of these, 15 show no apparent burst in amplification, and three haveÂ evidence of very recent (post-polyploid) activity (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig3">3</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">3</a>, and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>Teff belongs to the Chloridoideae subfamily of grasses, which includes important drought- and heat-tolerant C4 species such as the orphan grain crop finger millet and model desiccation tolerant plants in the genera <i>Oropetium</i>, <i>Eragrostis</i>, <i>Tripogon</i>, <i>Sporobolus</i>, and others. Most (~90%) of surveyed Chloridoideae species are polyploid, including many of the aforementioned taxa, and this likely contributes to their diversity and stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Roodt, R. &amp; Spies, J. J. Chromosome studies in the grass subfamily Chloridoideae. II. An analysis of polyploidy. Taxon 52, 736â746 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1509">17</a></sup>. We utilized the wealth of genomic resources within Chloridoideae and more generally across Poaceae to identify patterns associated with improved stress tolerance, polyploidy, and genome evolution in teff. The teff and Oropetium genomes have a high degree of collinearity, as demonstrated by highly conserved gene content and order along each chromosome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig4">4</a>). Teff and Oropetium show a clear 2:1 synteny pattern with 87% of teff genes having synteny to one block in Oropetium and 85% of Oropetium genes having synteny to two blocks in the teff genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig4">4a</a>). This ratio corresponds to the A and B homoeologs of tetraploid teff and the single orthologs of diploid Oropetium. Each Oropetium chromosome has clear collinearity to two homoeologous teff chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig4">4c</a>). Three trios have no rearrangements (teff 3A, 3B, and Oropetium Chr3; 4A, 4B, Chr4; 6A, 6B, Chr8) six trios have one or more large-scale inversions (1A, 1B, Chr1; 2A, 2B, Chr2; 5A, 5B, Chr7; 7A, 7B, Chr6; 8A, 8B, Chr9; 9A, 9B, Chr5) and one trio has translocations (10A, 10B, Chr10). Of the 28,909 Oropetium genes, 74% (21,293) have syntenic orthologs in both subgenomes of teff, 5% (1503) are found in only one subgenome, and 21% (6113) have no syntenic orthologs in teff. Teff and the allotetraploid grain crop finger millet have 2:2 synteny, but only 69% of syntenic blocks are found in duplicate because of the fragmented nature of the finger millet genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hittalmani, S. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequence of finger millet (Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.) provides insights into drought tolerance and nutraceutical properties. BMC Genomics 18, 465 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1523">26</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">4</a>). Only 56% (38,149) of the teff genes have two syntenic orthologs in finger millet and the remaining 13 and 30% (9228 and 20,878) have one or zero syntenic orthologs in finger millet, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Comparative genomics of the teff genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Comparative genomics of the teff genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="553" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Ratio of syntenic depth between Oropetium and teff. Syntenic blocks of Oropetium per teff gene (left) and syntenic blocks of teff per Oropetium gene (right) are shown indicating a clear 1:2 pattern of Oropetium to teff. <b>b</b> Microsynteny of the teff and Oropetium genomes. A region of the Oropetium chromosome 1 and the corresponding syntenic regions in homoeologous teff chromosomes 1A and 1B are shown. Genes are shown in red and blue (for forward and reverse orientation, respectively) and syntenic gene pairs are connected by gray lines. <b>c</b> Macrosynteny of the teff and Oropetium genomes. Syntenic gene pairs are denoted by gray points. <b>d</b> Collineariy of the teff subgenomes. The ten chromosomes belonging to the teff A and B subgenomes are shown in yellow and purple, respectively. Syntenic blocks between homoeologous regions are shown in grey. Source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">4c</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Following an allopolyploidy event, a dominant subgenome often emerges with significantly more retained genes and higher homoeolog expression as the plant returns to a diploid-like state<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934â946 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1556">27</a></sup>. This dominance is established immediately following the polyploidy event, and patterns of biased fractionation have been observed in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934â946 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1562">27</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069â4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1566">28</a></sup>, <i>Brassica rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1572">29</a></sup>, and bread wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li, A. et al. mRNA and small RNA transcriptomes reveal insights into dynamic homoeolog regulation of allopolyploid heterosis in nascent hexaploid wheat. Plant Cell 26, 1878â1900 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1577">30</a></sup>. Biased homoeolog loss (fractionation) is not universal, and other allopolyploids such as <i>Capsella bursa-pastoris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Douglas, G. M. et al. Hybrid origins and the earliest stages of diploidization in the highly successful recent polyploid Capsella bursa-pastoris. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 2806â2811 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1583">31</a></sup> and several Cucurbita species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293â1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1587">32</a></sup> display no subgenome dominance. We searched for biased fractionation using syntenic orthologs from Oropetium as anchors. The A and B subgenomes of teff have a near identical number of syntenic orthologs to Oropetium (21,697 vs. 21,520, respectively), suggesting that there is little or no biased fractionation in teff. Orthologs to 1325 Oropetium genes are found as single-copy loci in teff, including 647 and 678 from the A and B subgenomes, respectively. The remaining orthologs are maintained in duplicate in teff (21,276) compared to their single ortholog in Oropetium. Together, this suggests a general stability of gene content in <i>Eragrostis</i> after genome merger.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Homoeolog expression patterns and subgenome dominance</h3><p>To test for patterns of subgenome differentiation and dominance in teff, we surveyed gene expression in eight developmentally distinct tissue types and two stages of progressive drought stress. Sampled tissues include roots and shoots from seedlings and mature plants, internodes, and two stages of developing seeds. Tissue from mature, well-watered leaves and two time points of severe drought were also collected (leaf relative water content of 33% and 16%, respectively). Of the 23,303 syntenic gene pairs between the A and B subgenomes, 15,325 have homoeologous expression bias (HEB) in at least one tissue, and 1694 have biased expression in all sampled tissues (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">5</a>). Pairwise comparisons between syntenic gene pairs support a slight bias in transcript expression toward the B subgenome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig5">5a</a>). Roughly 56% of the 207,873 pairwise comparisons across the ten tissues show biased expression toward homoeologs in the B subgenome. This pattern is consistently observed across all ten tissues and most chromosome pairs, but the difference is subtle when robust cutoffs of differential expression are applied (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig5">5b, c</a>; see Methods). Individual tissues have from 6061 to 8485 homoeologous gene pairs with significant differential expression, including 52.3% biased toward the B subgenome (KruskalâWallis <i>H</i> test <i>P</i>â<i>&lt;</i>â0.01; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#Fig5">5b</a>). Eight pairs of chromosomes show HEB toward the B subgenome, and chromosomes 1 and 8 have more dominant homoeologs from the A subgenome, but the difference is not significant (Wilcoxonâs rank-sum <i>P</i>â&gt;â0.05). Together, this suggests that the B subgenome is universally dominant over the A subgenome, but when strict thresholds are applied, this difference is minimal. Although we detected no evidence of recent homoeologous exchange, it is possible that genes from the recessive genome were replaced with homoeologs from the dominant subgenome during polyploid formation, which would weaken patterns of subgenome dominance.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Homoeolog expression bias between the A and B subgenomes of teff."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Homoeolog expression bias between the A and B subgenomes of teff.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14724-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14724_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="542" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> The distribution of homoeolog expression bias (HEB) between all gene pairs in all tissues. An HEB &gt;0 indicates bias toward the A subgenome and a HEB &lt;0 indicates bias toward the B subgenome. <b>b</b> HEB across the ten tissues in the teff expression atlas. Gene pairs were classified as biased toward the A (blue) or B (red) subgenomes or balanced with no statistically significant differential expression (gray). <b>c</b> HEB in each of the ten pairs of chromosomes across all ten tissue types. Source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">5a</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>One of the reasons for teffâs status as the preferred cereal in Ethiopia and among many food nutritionists is the high quality and quantity of proteins in the teff grain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhang, W., Xu, J., Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Messing, J. Teff, an orphan cereal in the chloridoideae, provides insights into the evolution of storage proteins in grasses. Genome Biol. Evolution 8, 1712â1721 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1650">33</a></sup>. Because teff seeds are so small, analyzing gene expression during seed development is challenging. For this reason, our investigations of the expression of teff storage protein genes during seed development included several early stages where we did not attempt to separate the developing seed from surrounding flower tissues (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">6</a>). Hence, accurate quantification was not feasible, but analysis of the timing of expression was possible. The results indicated that the genes for all of the different classes of teff storage proteins, known as eragrostins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhang, W., Xu, J., Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Messing, J. Teff, an orphan cereal in the chloridoideae, provides insights into the evolution of storage proteins in grasses. Genome Biol. Evolution 8, 1712â1721 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1657">33</a></sup>, were primarily expressed during a narrow temporal window about 1 week after flower emergence (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">6</a>). As for many self-pollinated cereals, we expect that the pollination occurred before the flowers emerged from the stem at the boot leaf stage. For the most highly expressed storage protein genes (Î±-eragrostins on chromosomes 4A and 4B), there was a slight bias (53â47%) for expression from the A subgenome, while the next most highly expressed family for storage proteins (encoding the Î³-eragrostins on chromosomes 2A and 2B), there was also a bias (82â18%) towards expression from the A subgenome.</p><p>We tested whether gene pairs with HEB maintain patterns of dominance across all tissues or whether dominant homoeologs are reversed in different tissues or under stress. The vast majority of genes (86.9%; 13,322) with HEB maintain the same pattern of dominance across all tissues, while 13.1% (2002) of the gene pairs have opposite dominance patterns in different tissues. The remaining 7675 gene pairs have no expression bias in any tissues or both homoeologs have negligible expression. Severely dehydrated leaf tissue had the most gene pairs with HEB (36%; 8485) compared to seedling roots and shoots, which each had ~26% of pairs with HEB. These results are consistent with previous findings in allohexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="RamÃ­rez-GonzÃ¡lez, R. et al. The transcriptional landscape of polyploid wheat. Science 361, eaar6089 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1667">34</a></sup> and allotetraploid <i>Tragopogon mirus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Buggs, R. J. et al. Tissue-specific silencing of homoeologs in natural populations of the recent allopolyploid Tragopogon mirus. N. Phytologist 186, 175â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1673">35</a></sup>. We compared the ratio of nonsynonymous (Ka) to synonymous substitution rates (Ks) in homoeologous gene pairs to test if genes with stronger HEB are experiencing different patterns of selection. Gene pairs with stronger HEB had significantly higher Ka/Ks than gene pairs with no HEB in any tissue (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">7</a>; 0.17 vs. 0.28; MannâWhitney <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01). We detected no difference in divergence (Ks) among genes with varying degrees of HEB (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">8</a>). Based on this pattern, we hypothesize that homoeologous gene pairs with higher expression divergence are under more relaxed selective constraints than gene pairs with balanced expression.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Unlike the genomes of most polyploid grasses, the teff subgenomes are relatively small (~300âMb), with high gene density and low TE content. The subgenomes are highly syntenic along their length, with no evidence of major inversions or structural rearrangements, in contrast to patterns observed in other relatively recent allopolyploids such as canola (<i>Brassica napus</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1698">15</a></sup>, strawberry (<i>Fragaria ananassa</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Origin and evolution of the octoploid strawberry genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 541â547 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1705">16</a></sup>, and cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224â229 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1709">36</a></sup>. The cotton allotetraploid complex formed around the same time as teff (1.7â1.9âmya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1714">37</a></sup>, and the cotton A and D subgenomes diverged 6.2â7.1âmya. The two subgenomes have several hundred megabases of translocated sequences and strucural rearrangements. This same pattern of rearrangement is observed in the banana (<i>Musa balbisiana</i>) A and B subgenomes, which diverged ~5.4âmya and have several megabase pair sized translocations and inversions between them<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, Z. et al. Musa balbisiana genome reveals subgenome evolution and functional divergence. Nat. Plants 5, 810â821 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1721">38</a></sup>. The allohexaploid false flax (<i>Camelina stativa</i>) has evidence of shattered chromosomes with numerous rearrangements and fractionation of the subgenomes compared to the diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="MandÃ¡kovÃ¡, T., Pouch, M., Brock, J. R., Al-Shehbaz, I. A. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Origin and evolution of diploid and allopolyploid Camelina genomes was accompanied by chromosome shattering. Plant Cell 31, 2596â2612 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1728">39</a></sup>.</p><p>The general stability of the teff subgenomes may be attributed to low rates of homoeologous exchange. An estimated 90% of Chloridoid grasses are polyploid and among the allopolyploid species, multivalent pairing is rarely detected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Roodt, R. &amp; Spies, J. J. Chromosome studies in the grass subfamily Chloridoideae. II. An analysis of polyploidy. Taxon 52, 736â746 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1735">17</a></sup>. The twenty chromosome pairs in teff show bivalent pairing in meiosis I, and double reduction has not been observed in segregating populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yu, J.-K. et al. A genetic linkage map for tef [Eragrostis tef (Zucc.) Trotter]. Theor. Appl. Genet. 113, 1093â1102 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1739">20</a></sup>. Although homoeologous exchanges can result in advantageous emergent phenotypes, they can also destabilize the karyotype, leading to reduced fertility and fitness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous recombination in allopolyploids: the polyploid ratchet. N. Phytologist 186, 18â28 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1743">40</a></sup>. For this reason, recent polyploids have long been considered evolutionary dead ends<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mayrose, I. et al. Recently formed polyploid plants diversify at lower rates. Science 333, 1257â1257 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1747">41</a></sup>. Thus, proper bivalent pairing (disomic inheritance) in natural allopolyploids may be favored, and the near perfect synteny observed between teff subgenomes suggests that an underlying mechanism may exist to prevent or reduce homoeologous exchanges in this species. We detected no evidence of recent homoeologous exchange in teff based on Ks distribution in windows across the genome, including exchanges that would have happened at the inception of the polyploidy event ~1.1âmya. Homeologous exchanges are a common feature of allopolyploids, and the lack of these events is a unique feature of the teff genome.</p><p>The teff A and B subgenomes, and the Oropetium genome have high degrees of chromosome level collinearity despite their divergence. Oropetium and teff belong to different tribes within Chloridoideae (Cynodonteae and Eragrostideae, respectively) and diverged an estimated 25âmya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Christin, P.-A. et al. Oligocene CO2 decline promoted C4 photosynthesis in grasses. Curr. Biol. 18, 37â43 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1754">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Vicentini, A., Barber, J. C., Aliscioni, S. S., Giussani, L. M. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. The age of the grasses and clusters of origins of C4 photosynthesis. Glob. Change Biol. 14, 2963â2977 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1757">43</a></sup>. This is particularly unusual because polyploid-rich lineages typically have high rates of chromosome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wendel, J. F. in Plant Molecular Evolution (Springer, 2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1761">44</a></sup>. In contrast, our analysis of the divergence dates of the diploid A and B genome ancestors (~5âmya) and the formation of the tetraploid (~1.1âmya) indicates that the two genomes were so similar in structure (i.e., gene content, gene order and chromosome size) that some tetrasomic pairing would have been expected. Perhaps the <i>Ph1</i>-equivalent loci in <i>Eragrostis</i>, which control proper bivalent pairing in wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Riley, R. &amp; Chapman, V. Genetic control of the cytologically diploid behaviour of hexaploid wheat. Nature 182, 713â715 (1958)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1771">45</a></sup>, are so dominant that even low frequencies of homoeologous pairing are blocked. The high levels of subgenome compatibility, genetic and chromosome stability, fidelity for chromosome pairing, and low rates of homoeologous exchange allows polyploidy to dominate in the Chloridoideae subfamily. This polyploidy in turn may have enabled the emergent resilience and robustness observed in Chloridoid grasses.</p><p>Although we detected no biased fractionation between the teff subgenomes, we observed a general subgenome dominance across tissues in the expression atlas. The B subgenome is smaller and has fewer transposable elements, which may be contributing to the overall higher homoeolog expression levels. Patterns of B subgenome dominance are relatively weak compared to other allopolyploids, which may reflect the stability and lack of biased fractionation in teff. The teff subgenomes have successfully partitioned their ancestral roles, and most gene pairs display homoeolog expression bias. This bias is generally maintained across tissues and treatments, and few gene pairs change bias in a tissue-specific manner. Severely drought stressed leaf tissue has the highest proportion of genes with biased expression, which may reflect adaptation to adverse environments. Extensive homoeolog expression bias is also observed in hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="RamÃ­rez-GonzÃ¡lez, R. et al. The transcriptional landscape of polyploid wheat. Science 361, eaar6089 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1778">34</a></sup> and tetraploid <i>Tragopogon mirus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Buggs, R. J. et al. Tissue-specific silencing of homoeologs in natural populations of the recent allopolyploid Tragopogon mirus. N. Phytologist 186, 175â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1784">35</a></sup> and may be a common feature of recent polyploid grasses.</p><p>The vast majority of genes in teff are maintained as homoeologous gene pairs in the A and B subgenomes, providing a significant obstacle for targeted breeding. Efforts to produce semi-dwarf, lodging-resistant teff using a mutagenesis approach have been more difficult because of gene redundancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Zhu, Q. et al. High throughput discovery of mutations in tef semi-dwarfing genes by next generation sequencing analysis. Genetics 192, 819â829 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1792">46</a></sup>. The resources provided here will help accelerate marker-assisted selection and guide genome engineering-based approaches, which must take gene redundancy into account. Most gene pairs have divergent expression profiles such that the subgenomes likely contribute unequally to different agronomic traits. Teff is often described as an orphan grain crop because of its limited investigation and improvement, resulting in relatively low yields under ideal conditions compared to other cereals with intensive selection and breeding histories. Teff and other grasses within Chloridoideae have high tolerance to abiotic stresses, and most of this resilience was maintained during teff domestication. This may represent a historical alternative selection scheme where maximum yield is exchanged for reliable harvest under poor environmental conditions. Future efforts to improve food security during rapidly changing climates should utilize the natural resilience of these robust, stable, polyploid species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Plant materials</h3><p>The Dabbi cultivar of teff (PI 524438, <a href="http://www.ars-grin.gov">www.ars-grin.gov</a>) was chosen for sequencing. Plant materials for high-molecular-weight (HMW) genomic DNA extraction, Hi-C library construction, and RNA were maintained in growth chambers under a 12-h photoperiod with day/night temperatures of 28âÂ°C and 22âÂ°C, respectively, and a light intensity of 400âÎ¼Eâm<sup>â2</sup>âs<sup>â1</sup>. Tissue samples for the expression atlas were collected at ZT8 (Zeitgeber Time 8) to reduce issues associated with circadian oscillation. The Addisie cultivar of teff (PI 524434) was used for constructing the expression atlas. The tissue types used in the expression atlas include shoots and roots from young seedlings, mature leaf, internode, root, immature seeds, and mature seeds (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM1">9</a>). For the drought time points, mature teff plants were allowed to dry slowly and leaf tissue was collected at subsequent days of extreme drought when the plant tissues had 33% and 16% relative water content, as well as well-watered teff for comparison. Tissue for the seed development timepoints were collected from teff florets at six different stages from 1 week before to 5 weeks after flower emergence. Three biological replicates were collected for each sample for RNAseq expression analysis. Leaf tissue from seedlings was used for the HMW genomic DNA extraction and Hi-C library construction. Tissues for HMW genomic DNA extraction and RNAseq were immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at â80âÂ°C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">DNA library construction and sequencing</h3><p>HMW genomic DNA was isolated from young teff leaf tissue for both PacBio and Illumina sequencing. A modified nuclei preparation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Zhang, H. B., Zhao, X., Ding, X., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabaseâsize DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J. 7, 175â184 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1827">47</a></sup> was used to extract HMW genomic DNA and residual contaminants were removed using phenol chloroform purification. PacBio libraries were constructed using the manufacturerâs protocol and were size selected for 30âkb fragments on the BluePippen system (Sage Science), followed by subsequent purification using AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter). The PacBio libraries were sequenced on a PacBio RSII system with P6C4 chemistry. In total, 5.5 million filtered PacBio reads were generated, collectively spanning 52.9âGb or ~85Ã genome coverage (assuming a genome size of 622âMb). The same batch of HMW genomic DNA was used to construct Illumina DNAseq libraries for correcting residual errors in the PacBio assembly. Libraries were constructed using the KAPA HyperPrep Kit (Kapa Biosystems), followed by sequencing on an Illumina HiSeq4000 under paired-end mode (150âbp).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">RNA extraction and library construction</h3><p>RNA for the expression atlas was extracted using the Omega Biotek E.Z.N.A.<sup>Â®</sup> Plant RNA Kit according to the manufacturerâs protocol. Roughly 200âmg of ground tissue was used for each extraction. The RNA quality was validated using gel electrophoresis and the Qubit RNA IQ Assay (Thermo Fisher). Stranded RNAseq libraries were constructed using 2âÎ¼g of total RNA quantified using the Qubit RNA HS Assay Kit (Invitrogen, USA) with the Illumina TruSeq stranded total RNA LT Sample Prep Kit (RS-122-2401 and RS-122-2402). Multiplexed libraries were pooled and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq4000 under paired-end 150ânt mode. Three replicates were sequenced for each timepoint/sample.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>The genome size of Dabbi teff was estimated using flow cytometry as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Estimation of nuclear DNA content of plants by flow cytometry. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 229â241 (1991)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1849">48</a></sup>. The estimated flow cytometry size was 622âMb, which was consistent with kmer-based estimations from Illumina data. The kmer plot had a unimodal distribution suggesting low within genome heterozygosity and high differentiation from the teff A and B subgenomes. Raw PacBio data was error corrected and assembled using Canu (v1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1853">18</a></sup>, which produced accurate and contiguous assembly for homozygous plant genomes. The following parameters were modified: minReadLengthâ=â2000, GenomeSizeâ=â622âMb, minOverlapLengthâ=â1000. Assembly graphs were visualized after each iteration of Canu in Bandage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wick, R. R., Schultz, M. B., Zobel, J. &amp; Holt, K. E. Bandage: interactive visualization of de novo genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 31, 3350â3352 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1857">49</a></sup> to assess complexities related to repetitive elements and homoeologous regions. The final Canu-based PacBio assembly has a contig N50 of 1.55âMb across 1344 contigs with a total assembly size of 576âMb. The raw PacBio contigs were polished to remove residual errors with Pilon (v1.22)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PloS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1861">19</a></sup> using 73Ã coverage of Illumina paired-end 150âbp data. Illumina reads were quality-trimmed using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1865">50</a></sup>, followed by aligning to the assembly with bowtie2 (v2.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1870">51</a></sup> under default parameters. Parameters for Pilon were modified as follows: --flank 7, --K 49, and --mindepth 15. Pilon was run recursively three times using the modified corrected assembly after each round. Ten full-length fosmids (collectively spanning 351âkb) were aligned to the final PacBio assembly to assess the quality. The fosmids exhibited an average identity of 99.9% to the PacBio assembly, with individual fosmids ranging from 99.3 to 100% nucleotide identity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genetic map construction</h3><p>A previously generated recombinant inbred population derived from an interspecific cross of <i>E. tef</i> and <i>E. pilosa</i> was used to generate a high-density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yu, J.-K. et al. A genetic linkage map for tef [Eragrostis tef (Zucc.) Trotter]. Theor. Appl. Genet. 113, 1093â1102 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1889">20</a></sup>. GBS libraries were constructed with the ApeKI enzyme and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2000 sequencer under paired-end mode. The GBS reads were analyzed using teff contig assemblies as a reference, with the TASSEL-GBS pipeline (v4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Glaubitz, J. C. et al. TASSEL-GBS: a high capacity genotyping by sequencing analysis pipeline. PLoS ONE 9, e90346 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1893">52</a></sup>. Highly informative SNP markers (present in &gt;80% of plants) were used for map construction. The genetic map was constructed using Joinmap (version 4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stam, P. Construction of integrated genetic linkage maps by means of a new computer package: Join Map. Plant J. 3, 739â744 (1993)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1897">53</a></sup> and Mapmaker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lander, E. S. et al. MAPMAKER: an interactive computer package for constructing primary genetic linkage maps of experimental and natural populations. Genomics 1, 174â181 (1987)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1902">54</a></sup>, using the Haldane function and a regression algorithm.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Hi-C analysis and pseudomolecule construction</h3><p>The PacBio-based teff contigs were anchored into a chromosome-scale assembly using a Hi-C proximity-based assembly approach as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="VanBuren, R., Wai, C. M., Keilwagen, J. &amp; Pardo, J. A chromosome-scale assembly of the model desiccation tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Plant Direct 2, e00096 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1914">24</a></sup>. A Hi-C library was constructed using 0.2âg of leaf tissue collected from newly emerged teff seedlings with the Proximoâ¢ Hi-C Plant Kit (Phase Genomics) following the manufacturerâs protocol. After verifying quality, the Hi-C library was size selected for 300â600âbp fragments and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq4000 under paired-end 150âbp mode. One hundred and fifty base pair reads were used to avoid erroneous alignment in highly similar homoeologous regions. In total, 226 million read pairs were used as input for the Juicer and 3d-DNA Hi-C analysis and scaffolding pipelines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst. 3, 95â98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1918">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92â95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1921">56</a></sup>. Illumina reads were quality-trimmed using Trimmomatic (0.39)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1925">50</a></sup> and aligned to the contigs using BWA (v0.7.16)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;http://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1929">57</a></sup> with strict parameters (-n 0) to prevent mismatches and non-specific alignments in repetitive and homoeologous regions. Contigs were ordered and oriented and assembly errors were identified using the 3d-DNA pipeline with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92â95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1933">56</a></sup>. The resulting Hi-C contact matrix was visualized using Juicebox, and misassemblies and misjoins were manually corrected based on neighboring interactions. This approach identified 20 high-confidence clusters representing the haploid chromosome number in teff. The manually validated assembly was used to build pseudomolecules using the finalize-output.sh script from 3d-DNA and chromosomes were renamed and ordered by size and binned to the A and B subgenomes based on centromeric array analysis (described in detail below).</p><p>The accuracy of the Hi-C-based pseudomolecules was assessed using a high-density SNP-based genetic map with 2002 markers across 32 linkage groups. Several chromosomes were broken into multiple linkage groups in this map because of low population size. SNP-based markers were mapped to the teff genome using BLAST and collinearity between the physical and genetic map was assessed using the ALLMAPS package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1940">58</a></sup>. The small differences between the pseudomolecules and genetic map are likely driven by missing data and marker distortion as well as the interspecific nature of this mapping population (<i>E. tef</i>âÃâ<i>E. pilosa</i>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Identification of repetitive elements</h3><p>We first identified and masked the simple sequence repeats in the teff genome with GMATA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Wang, X. &amp; Wang, L. GMATA: an integrated software package for genome-scale SSR mining, marker development and viewing. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1958">59</a></sup>, and then conducted structure-based full-length TE identification using the following bioinformatic tools: LTR_retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410â1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1962">60</a></sup> to acquire high-confidence full LTR-RTs, SINE-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Wenke, T. et al. Targeted identification of short interspersed nuclear element families shows their widespread existence and extreme heterogeneity in plant genomes. Plant Cell 23, 3117â3128 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1966">61</a></sup> to identify SINEs, MGEscan-nonLTR (v2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Rho, M. &amp; Tang, H. MGEScan-non-LTR: computational identification and classification of autonomous non-LTR retrotransposons in eukaryotic genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, e143 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1970">62</a></sup> to identify LINEs, MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1974">63</a></sup> and MITE Tracker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Crescente, J. M., Zavallo, D., Helguera, M. &amp; Vanzetti, L. S. MITE Tracker: an accurate approach to identify miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements in large genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 348 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1979">64</a></sup> to identify TIRs, and HelitronScanner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Xiong, W., He, L., Lai, J., Dooner, H. K. &amp; Du, C. HelitronScanner uncovers a large overlooked cache of Helitron transposons in many plant genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 10263â10268 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1983">65</a></sup> to identify <i>Helitrons</i>. All TEs were classified and manually checked according to the nomenclature system of transposons as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d58041e1990">66</a></sup> and against Repbase to validate their annotation. We used the newly identified TEs as a custom library to identify full-length and truncated TE elements through a homology-based search with RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>, version 4.0.7) using the teff pseudomolecules as input. The distribution of repeat sequences was then calculated. Only LTR-RT families with at least five intact copies were used for analysis of subgenome specificity. Within the 65 families having &gt;5 intact elements, we identified LTRs with subgenomic specific activity. A family is considered as subgenomic specific if all intact elements of this family are from the same subgenome. Subgenome specificity was verified through BLAST of the element against the genome, and the distribution of matched sequences was manually inspected for subgenome specificity. The approximate insertion dates of LTR-RTs were calculated using the evolutionary distance between two LTR-RTs with the formula of <i>T</i>â=â<i>K</i>/2<i>Î¼</i>, where <i>K</i> is the divergence rate approximated by percent identity and <i>Î¼</i> is the neutral mutation rate estimated as <i>Î¼</i>â=â1.3âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> mutations per bp per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B. S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43â45 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2020">23</a></sup>.</p><p>Putative centromeric repeat arrays were identified with the approach outlined in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Melters, D. P. et al. Comparative analysis of tandem repeats from hundreds of species reveals unique insights into centromere evolution. Genome Biol. 14, R10 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2027">67</a></sup> using Tandem repeat finder (version 4.07)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2031">68</a></sup>. Parameters were modified as follows for tandem repeat finder: â1 1 2 80 5 200 2000 -d âh.â Centromere-specific repeats are often the most abundant tandem repeats in the genome, and they were identified in teff by the following criteria: (1) copy number, (2) sequence level conservation between chromosomes, (3) similarity to other grass repeats, and (4) proximity to centromere-specific <i>gypsy</i> LTR-RTs. This approach identified two distinct centromere-specific arrays (SatTA and SatTB) with a shared length of 159âbp yet distinct sequence compositions. The consensus sequence of centromeric repeats from each chromosome was used to construct a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree implemented in MEGA5 (v10.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2038">69</a></sup>. This approach separated centromeric repeats from the 20 chromosomes into two distinct groups corresponding to the A and B subgenomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome annotation</h3><p>Genes in the teff genome were annotated using the MAKER-P pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513â524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2050">70</a></sup>. The LTR-RT repeat library from LTR retriever was used for repeat masking. Transcript-based evidence was generated using RNAseq data from the ten tissues of the teff expression atlas. Quality-trimmed RNAseq reads were aligned to the unmasked teff genome using the splice aware alignment program STAR (v2.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15â21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2054">71</a></sup> and transcripts were identified using StringTie (v1.3.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2058">72</a></sup> with default parameters. The âmerge flag was used to combine the output from individual libraries to generate a representative set of non-redundant transcripts. Protein sequences from the <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice, and sorghum genomes as well as proteins from the UniProtKB plant databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Boutet, E., Lieberherr, D., Tognolli, M., Schneider, M. &amp; Bairoch A. in Plant Bioinformatics (Springer, 2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2065">73</a></sup> were used as protein evidence. Ab initio gene prediction was conducted using SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2070">74</a></sup> and Augustus (3.0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, ii215âii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2074">75</a></sup> with two rounds of iterative training. The resulting gene models were filtered to remove any residual repetitive elements using BLAST with a non-redundant transposase library. The annotation quality was assessed using the BUSCO v.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2078">76</a></sup> with the plant-specific dataset (embryophyta_odb9).</p><p>Missing homeologous genes were identified using the set of low confidence gene models that were purged from the final annotation because of insufficient evidence or characteristics of pseudogenes. BLAST was also used to identify any genes that were fragmented, pseudogenized, or were otherwise missed in the annotation. In total, evidence was found for 9036 homeologous genes that were annotated as missing from one subgenome. Many of these genes had premature stop codons, were missing exons, or had no detectable expression, so most of these are presumably pseudogenes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNAseq expression analysis and homoeolog expression bias</h3><p>Gene expression levels were quantified with the pseudo-aligner Kallisto (v0.44.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Bray, N. L., Pimentel, H., Melsted, P. &amp; Pachter, L. Near-optimal probabilistic RNA-seq quantification. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 525â527 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2093">77</a></sup> using the teff gene models as a reference. Paired-end Illumina reads from the ten tissues in the expression atlas were quality trimmed using Trimmomatic (v0.33) with default parameters and pseudo-aligned to the gene models with Kallisto under default parameters with 100 bootstraps per sample. The teff A and B subgenomes have high sequence divergence (~7%) such that misalignment between homoeologs was minimal. Expression levels were quantified as transcripts per million and the three biological replicates were averaged for direct homoeolog comparisons.</p><p>HEB was identified across all 1:1 homeologous gene pairs using DESeq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2100">78</a></sup> for all 10 samples in the teff expression atlas. Gene pairs were classified as having HEB if they passed the threshold of differential expression in DESeq2 using the following model (model 1):</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${yij}\;{\mathrm{\sim }}\;{\mu}\;{\mathrm{ + }}\;{\mathrm{timepoint}}\;{\mathrm{ + }}\;{eij}.$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><p>Comparisons were made between the two homeologous gene pairs for each of the ten samples in the atlas. The built-in Wald test in the DEseq2 package was used to test whether the logâ2 fold change of a given gene was equal to 0 and genes with an false discovery rate-corrected, <i>p</i> value &lt;0.05 were considered differentially expressed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Comparative genomics</h3><p>Homoeologous gene pairs between the teff A and B subgenomes and syntenic gene pairs across select grasses were identified using the MCSCAN toolkit implemented in python [<a href="https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan">https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan</a>-(Python-version)]. Teff homoeologs were identified by all vs. all alignment using LAST, and hits were filtered using default parameters in MCSCAN with a minimum block size of five genes. Retained gene pairs from the <i>Ï</i> and <i>Ï</i> whole-genome duplication events were filtered out using a C-score cutoff of 0.99. This approach identified 23,303 homoeologous, syntenic gene pairs between the A and B subgenome. Homoeolougs gene pairs with translocations were not identified using this syntenic approach and were thus excluded from analysis. Tandem gene duplicates in teff were identified from the all vs. all LAST output with a maximum gene distance of 10. Gene models from teff were aligned to the <i>Oropetium thomaeum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="VanBuren, R., Wai, C. M., Keilwagen, J. &amp; Pardo, J. A chromosome-scale assembly of the model desiccation tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Plant Direct 2, e00096 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2159">24</a></sup> and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> genes as outlined above for comparative genomics analyses across grasses. Macro and microsyntenic dot plots, block depths, and karyotype comparisons were generated in python using scripts from MSCAN.</p><p>Ka and Ks values were computed using a set of custom scripts available on GitHub: [<a href="https://github.com/Aeyocca/ka_ks_pipe/">https://github.com/Aeyocca/ka_ks_pipe/</a>]. The homoeologous gene pair list from the teff subgenomes and syntenic orthologs between teff and Oropetium were used as input and the protein sequences from each gene pair were aligned using MUSCLE v3.8.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2173">79</a></sup>. PAL2NAL (v14)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609âW612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d58041e2177">80</a></sup> was used to convert the peptide alignment to a nucleotide alignment and Ks values were computed between gene pairs using codeml from PAML (v4.9h) with parameters specified in the control file found in the GitHub repository listed above. Regions with recent homeologous exchanges were identified by comparing Ks values of syntenic gene pairs along windows across the genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>

RNAseq reads from the teff expression atlas were deposited to the National Center for Biotechnology Information Short Read Archive under bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA525065">PRJNA525065</a>. The genome assembly and annotation for teff is available from CoGe under <a href="https://genomevolution.org/coge/GenomeInfo.pl?gid=50954">id50954</a>. The UniProtKB plant databases [<a href="https://www.uniprot.org/help/plants">https://www.uniprot.org/help/plants</a>] and embryophyta_odb9 BUSCO dataset [<a href="https://busco-archive.ezlab.org/v2/datasets/embryophyta_odb9.tar.gz">https://busco-archive.ezlab.org/v2/datasets/embryophyta_odb9.tar.gz</a>] were downloaded from source for data analyses. The source data underlying Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">4</a>c, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">5a</a> and Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">4</a>a, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">7</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14724-z#MOESM4">8</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>

              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">
                <i>PeerJ</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
